<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juden_1881.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Juden_1881.JPG/300px-Juden_1881.JPG" decoding="async" width="300" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Juden_1881.JPG/450px-Juden_1881.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Juden_1881.JPG/600px-Juden_1881.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4782" data-file-height="3936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juden_1881.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Jews in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> (1881)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Ashkenazi Jews</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span></span>-, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">ASH</span>-, <span style="font-size:90%">AHSH</span>-kə-<span style="font-size:90%">NAH</span>-zee</i></a><sup id="cite_ref-longman_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>), also known as <b>Ashkenazic Jews</b> or, by using the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> plural suffix -im, <b>Ashkenazim</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">æ</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span></span>-, <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="/ʃ/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">ʃ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">ASH</span>-, <span style="font-size:90%">AHSH</span>-kə-<span style="font-size:90%">NAH</span>-zim</i></a>;<sup id="cite_ref-longman_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049626">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-size:1.15em;font-family:"Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey David CLM","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli","SBL BibLit","SBL Hebrew",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">אַשְׁכְּנַזִּים</span>, <small>Ashkenazi Hebrew pronunciation:</small> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[ˌaʃkəˈnazim]</a></span>, singular: <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[ˌaʃkəˈnazi]</a></span>, <small>Modern Hebrew:</small> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">[aʃkenaˈzim, aʃkenaˈzi]</a></span>; also <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049626"/><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">יְהוּדֵי אַשְׁכְּנַז</span> <i><i lang="he-Latn" title="Hebrew-language romanization">Y'hudey Ashkenaz</i></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> are a <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">Jewish diaspora population</a> who <a href="/wiki/Coalescent_theory" title="Coalescent theory">coalesced</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> around the end of the <a href="/wiki/First_millennium" class="mw-redirect" title="First millennium">first millennium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mosk_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mosk-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional diaspora language of Ashkenazi Jews is <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic language</a> with elements of <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Mosk_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mosk-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> developed after they had moved into northern Europe: beginning with Germany and France in the Middle Ages. For centuries they used <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> only as a <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">sacred language</a>, until <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">the revival of Hebrew as a common language</a> in Israel. Throughout their time in Europe, Ashkenazim have made many important contributions to its philosophy, scholarship, literature, art, music and science.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Ashkenazi" refers to Jewish settlers who established communities along the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> river in Western Germany and in Northern France dating to the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Once there, they adapted traditions carried from Babylon, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, and the Western Mediterranean to their new environment.<sup id="cite_ref-shum_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shum-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Ashkenazi religious rite developed in cities such as <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Troyes" title="Troyes">Troyes</a>. The eminent French <i><a href="/wiki/Rishonim" title="Rishonim">Rishon</a></i> rabbi Shlomo Itzhaki (<a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a>) would have a significant influence on the Jewish religion.
</p><p>In the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, due to religious persecution, the majority of the Ashkenazi population shifted steadily eastward,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> moving out of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> into the areas later part of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>, comprising parts of present-day <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the course of the late 18th and 19th centuries, those Jews who remained in or returned to the German lands generated a cultural reorientation; under the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">Haskalah</a> and the struggle for emancipation, as well as the intellectual and cultural ferment in urban centers, they gradually abandoned the use of Yiddish and adopted <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, while developing new forms of Jewish religious life and cultural identity.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">The Holocaust</a> of the Second World War decimated the Ashkenazim, affecting almost every Jewish family.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that in the 11th century Ashkenazi Jews composed three percent of the world's <a href="/wiki/Jewish_population_by_country" title="Jewish population by country">total Jewish population</a>, while an estimate made in 1930 (near the population's peak) had them as 92 percent of the world's Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Immediately prior to the Holocaust, the number of Jews in the world stood at approximately 16.7 million.<sup id="cite_ref-JVIL2010_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVIL2010-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Statistical figures vary for the contemporary demography of Ashkenazi Jews, ranging from 10 million<sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenazi-jews-hugr_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenazi-jews-hugr-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> to 11.2 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Jhu.edu_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jhu.edu-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sergio_Della_Pergola" title="Sergio Della Pergola">Sergio Della Pergola</a>, in a rough calculation of <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>, implies that Ashkenazi Jews make up less than 74% of Jews worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com.au_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com.au-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Other estimates place Ashkenazi Jews as making up about 75% of Jews worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_studies_on_Jews" title="Genetic studies on Jews">Genetic studies on Ashkenazim</a>&#8212;researching both their paternal and maternal lineages&#8212;suggest that they are of both Middle Eastern and European descent. These studies have arrived at diverging conclusions regarding both the degree and the sources of their European and Middle Eastern ancestry, with some focusing on the extent of the European genetic origin observed in Ashkenazi maternal lineages.<sup id="cite_ref-Summary_of_Recent_Genetic_Studies_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summary_of_Recent_Genetic_Studies-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Ashkenazi Jews are popularly contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardi Jews</a> (also called Sephardim), who descend from Jews who settled in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>, who descend from Jews who remained in the Middle East.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The name <i>Ashkenazi</i> derives from the biblical figure of <a href="/wiki/Ashkenaz" title="Ashkenaz">Ashkenaz</a>, the first son of <a href="/wiki/Gomer" title="Gomer">Gomer</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Japhet" class="mw-redirect" title="Japhet">Japhet</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Japhetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Japhetic">Japhetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Table_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Table of Nations">Table of Nations</a> (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#Chapter_10" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 10</a>).
The name of Gomer has often been linked to the ethnonym <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>.
</p><p>Biblical <i>Ashkenaz</i> is usually derived from <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Assyrian</a> <i>Aškūza</i> (<a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> <i>Aškuzai/Iškuzai</i>), a people who expelled the Cimmerians from the Armenian area of the Upper <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Gmirkin_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gmirkin-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> whose name is usually associated with the name of the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bøe_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bøe-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vladimir_Shneider_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vladimir_Shneider-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The intrusive <i>n</i> in the Biblical name is likely due to a scribal error confusing a <i>vav</i> <a href="/wiki/Vav_(letter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vav (letter)"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049626"/><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">ו</span></a> with a <i>nun</i> <a href="/wiki/Nun_(letter)" title="Nun (letter)"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049626"/><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">נ</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vladimir_Shneider_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vladimir_Shneider-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kriwaczek_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kriwaczek-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jeremiah" title="Book of Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a> 51:27, Ashkenaz figures as one of three kingdoms in the far north, the others being <a href="/wiki/Mannaeans" title="Mannaeans">Minni</a> and Ararat, perhaps corresponding to <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a>, called on by God to resist Babylon.<sup id="cite_ref-Kriwaczek_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kriwaczek-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Yoma" title="Yoma">Yoma</a> tractate of the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</a> the name Gomer is rendered as <i>Germania</i>, which elsewhere in rabbinical literature was identified with <a href="/wiki/Kahramanmara%C5%9F" title="Kahramanmaraş">Germanikia</a> in northwestern Syria, but later became associated with <i>Germania</i>. Ashkenaz is linked to <a href="/wiki/Scandza" title="Scandza">Scandza/Scanzia</a>, viewed as the cradle of Germanic tribes, as early as a 6th-century gloss to the <a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Historia Ecclesiastica</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenaz_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenaz-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 10th-century <i>History of Armenia</i> of <a href="/wiki/Hovhannes_Draskhanakerttsi" title="Hovhannes Draskhanakerttsi">Yovhannes Drasxanakertc'i</a> (1.15) Ashkenaz was associated with <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> as it was occasionally in Jewish usage, where its denotation extended at times to <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khazaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Khazaria">Khazaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> and areas to the east.<sup id="cite_ref-Poliak_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poliak-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> His contemporary <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">Saadia Gaon</a> identified Ashkenaz with the <i>Saquliba</i> or <a href="/wiki/East_Slavs" title="East Slavs">Slavic territories</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Malkiel_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malkiel-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and such usage covered also the lands of tribes neighboring the Slavs, and Eastern and Central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Poliak_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Poliak-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In modern times, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Krauss" title="Samuel Krauss">Samuel Krauss</a> identified the Biblical "Ashkenaz" with <a href="/wiki/Khazaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Khazaria">Khazaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometime in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Medieval">Early Medieval</a> period, the Jews of central and eastern Europe came to be called by this term.<sup id="cite_ref-Kriwaczek_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kriwaczek-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Conforming to the custom of designating areas of Jewish settlement with biblical names, Spain was denominated <i>Sefarad</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Obadiah" title="Book of Obadiah">Obadiah</a> 20), France was called <i>Tsarefat</i> (<a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">1 Kings 17:9</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> was called the <i><a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Land of Canaan</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/High_medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="High medieval">high medieval</a> period, Talmudic commentators like <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a> began to use <i>Ashkenaz/Eretz Ashkenaz</i> to designate <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Germany">Germany</a>, earlier known as <i><a href="/wiki/Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">Loter</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kriwaczek_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kriwaczek-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenaz_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenaz-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> where, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> communities of <a href="/wiki/Speyer" title="Speyer">Speyer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>, the most important Jewish communities arose.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Rashi uses <i>leshon Ashkenaz</i> (Ashkenazi language) to describe German speech, and Byzantium and Syrian Jewish letters referred to the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusaders</a> as Ashkenazim.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenaz_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenaz-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Given the close links between the Jewish communities of France and Germany following the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian unification</a>, the term Ashkenazi came to refer to the Jews of both medieval Germany and France.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Jews_in_Europe_before_the_Ashkenazim">History of Jews in Europe before the Ashkenazim</span></h3>
<p>Outside of their origins in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Israel">ancient Israel</a>, the history of Ashkenazim is shrouded in mystery,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and many theories have arisen speculating on their emergence as a distinct community of Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Weinryb_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinryb-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The best supported theory is the one that details a Jewish migration from Israel through what is now <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and other parts of southern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The historical record attests to Jewish communities in southern Europe since pre-Christian times.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Many Jews were denied full <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">Roman citizenship</a> until Emperor <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitutio_Antoniniana" title="Constitutio Antoniniana">granted</a> all free peoples this privilege in 212. Jews were required to pay a <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax" title="Poll tax">poll tax</a> until the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian</a> in 363. In the late Roman Empire, Jews were free to form networks of cultural and religious ties and enter into various local occupations. But, after Christianity became the official religion of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 380, Jews were increasingly marginalized.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Greece" title="History of the Jews in Greece">history of Jews in Greece</a> goes back to at least the <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic Era of Greece</a>, when the classical culture of Greece was undergoing a process of formalization after the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Dark Age">Greek Dark Age</a>. The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> knew of the Jews, whom he called "Palestinian Syrians",<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and listed them among the levied naval forces in service of the invading <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persians</a>. While Jewish monotheism was not deeply affected by Greek polytheism, the Greek way of living was attractive for many wealthier Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Synagogue_in_the_Agora_of_Athens" title="Synagogue in the Agora of Athens">Synagogue in the Agora of Athens</a> is dated to the period between 267 and 396 CE. The Stobi Synagogue in <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Macedonia</a> was built on the ruins of a more ancient synagogue in the 4th century, while later in the 5th century, the synagogue was transformed into a Christian basilica.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> thrived in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, many of these <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Koine_Greek" title="Jewish Koine Greek">Greek-speaking</a> Jews who would convert to Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sporadic<sup id="cite_ref-Mócsy_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mócsy-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphic evidence</a> in gravesite excavations, particularly in Brigetio (<a href="/wiki/Sz%C5%91ny" title="Szőny">Szőny</a>), Aquincum (<a href="/wiki/%C3%93buda" title="Óbuda">Óbuda</a>), Intercisa (<a href="/wiki/Duna%C3%BAjv%C3%A1ros" title="Dunaújváros">Dunaújváros</a>), Triccinae (<a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1rv%C3%A1r" title="Sárvár">Sárvár</a>), Savaria (<a href="/wiki/Szombathely" title="Szombathely">Szombathely</a>), Sopianae (<a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9cs" title="Pécs">Pécs</a>) in Hungary, and Mursa (<a href="/wiki/Osijek" title="Osijek">Osijek</a>) in Croatia, attest to the presence of Jews after the 2nd and 3rd centuries where Roman garrisons were established,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> There was a sufficient number of Jews in <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a> to form communities and build a synagogue. Jewish troops were among the Syrian soldiers transferred there, and replenished from the Middle East, after 175 CE Jews and especially Syrians came from <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>. Others came from Italy and the Hellenized parts of the Roman Empire. The excavations suggest they first lived in isolated enclaves attached to Roman legion camps and intermarried with other similar oriental families within the military orders of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Mócsy_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mócsy-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Patai" title="Raphael Patai">Raphael Patai</a> states that later Roman writers remarked that they differed little in either customs, manner of writing, or names from the people among whom they dwelt; and it was especially difficult to differentiate Jews from the Syrians.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> After Pannonia was ceded to the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a> in 433, the garrison populations were withdrawn to Italy, and only a few, enigmatic traces remain of a possible Jewish presence in the area some centuries later.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> No evidence has yet been found of a Jewish presence in antiquity in Germany beyond its Roman border, nor in Eastern Europe. In Gaul and Germany itself, with the possible exception of <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Cologne" title="History of the Jews in Cologne">Cologne</a>, the archeological evidence suggests at most a fleeting presence of very few Jews, primarily itinerant traders or artisans.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimating the number of Jews in antiquity is a task fraught with peril due to the nature of and lack of accurate documentation. The number of Jews in the Roman Empire for a long time was based on the accounts of Syrian Orthodox bishop <a href="/wiki/Bar_Hebraeus" title="Bar Hebraeus">Bar Hebraeus</a> who lived between 1226 and 1286 CE, who stated by the time of the destruction of the Second Temple in 70 CE, as many as six million Jews were already living in the Roman Empire, a conclusion which has been contested as highly exaggerated. The 13th-century author Bar Hebraeus gave a figure of 6,944,000 Jews in the Roman world. <a href="/wiki/Salo_Wittmayer_Baron" title="Salo Wittmayer Baron">Salo Wittmayer Baron</a> considered the figure convincing.<sup id="cite_ref-Baron_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baron-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The figure of seven million within and one million outside the Roman world in the mid-first century became widely accepted, including by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Feldman" title="Louis Feldman">Louis Feldman</a>. However, contemporary scholars now accept that Bar Hebraeus based his figure on a census of total Roman citizens and thus included non-Jews, the figure of 6,944,000 being recorded in <a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Eusebius)" title="Chronicon (Eusebius)">Eusebius' <i>Chronicon</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> 90, 94, 104–05</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rutgers_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rutgers-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Louis Feldman, previously an active supporter of the figure, now states that he and Baron were mistaken.<sup id="cite_ref-Feldman_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> 185</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philo" title="Philo">Philo</a> gives a figure of one million Jews living in Egypt. John R. Bartlett rejects Baron's figures entirely, arguing that we have no clue as to the size of the Jewish demographic in the ancient world.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span> 97–103</span></sup> The Romans did not distinguish between Jews inside and outside of the land of Israel/Judaea. They collected an annual <a href="/wiki/Temple_tax" title="Temple tax">temple tax</a> from Jews both in and outside of Israel. The revolts in and suppression of diaspora communities in Egypt, Libya and Crete in 115–117 CE had a severe impact on the Jewish diaspora.
</p><p>A substantial Jewish population emerged in northern Gaul by the Middle Ages,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> but Jewish communities existed in 465 CE in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, in 524 CE in <a href="/wiki/Valence_(city)" title="Valence (city)">Valence</a>, and in 533 CE in <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Orléans">Orléans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Throughout this period and into the early Middle Ages, some Jews assimilated into the dominant Greek and Latin cultures, mostly through conversion to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2014)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> King <a href="/wiki/Dagobert_I" title="Dagobert I">Dagobert I</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> expelled the Jews from his <a href="/wiki/Merovingian" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingian">Merovingian</a> kingdom in 629. Jews in former Roman territories faced new challenges as harsher anti-Jewish church rulings were enforced.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>'s expansion of the Frankish empire around 800, including northern Italy and Rome, brought on a brief period of stability and unity in <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a>. This created opportunities for Jewish merchants to settle again north of the Alps. Charlemagne granted the Jews freedoms similar to those once enjoyed under the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. In addition, Jews from southern Italy, fleeing religious persecution, began to move into central Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Returning to Frankish lands, many Jewish merchants took up occupations in finance and commerce, including money lending, or <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a>. (Church legislation banned Christians from lending money in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Interest" title="Interest">interest</a>.) From Charlemagne's time to the present, Jewish life in northern Europe is well documented. By the 11th century, when <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Troyes" title="Troyes">Troyes</a> wrote his commentaries, Jews in what came to be known as "Ashkenaz" were known for their <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakhic learning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmudic studies</a>. They were criticized by <a href="/wiki/Sephardim" class="mw-redirect" title="Sephardim">Sephardim</a> and other Jewish scholars in Islamic lands for their lack of expertise in Jewish jurisprudence and general ignorance of Hebrew linguistics and literature.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (April 2019)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ashkenazi_Jews#Dubious" title="Talk:Ashkenazi Jews">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish</a> emerged as a result of <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Latin" title="Judeo-Latin">Judeo-Latin</a> language contact with various <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">High German</a> <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernaculars</a> in the medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> It is a Germanic language written in Hebrew letters, and heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>, with some elements of <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_and_Late_Middle_Ages_migrations">High and Late Middle Ages migrations</span></h3>
<p>Historical records show evidence of Jewish communities north of the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> as early as the 8th and 9th century. By the 11th century Jewish settlers, moving from southern European and Middle Eastern centers, appear to have begun to settle in the north, especially along the Rhine, often in response to new economic opportunities and at the invitation of local Christian rulers. Thus <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_V,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Baldwin V, Count of Flanders">Baldwin V, Count of Flanders</a>, invited Jacob ben Yekutiel and his fellow Jews to settle in his lands; and soon after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> likewise extended a welcome to continental Jews to take up residence there. Bishop <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Speyer" title="History of the Jews in Speyer">Rüdiger Huzmann</a> called on the Jews of <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a> to relocate to <a href="/wiki/Speyer" title="Speyer">Speyer</a>. In all of these decisions, the idea that Jews had the know-how and capacity to jump-start the economy, improve revenues, and enlarge trade seems to have played a prominent role.<sup id="cite_ref-Rowe_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Typically Jews relocated close to the markets and churches in town centres, where, though they came under the authority of both royal and ecclesiastical powers, they were accorded administrative autonomy.<sup id="cite_ref-Rowe_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 11th century, both <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a> and the culture of the Babylonian Talmud that underlies it became established in southern Italy and then spread north to Ashkenaz.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous massacres of Jews occurred throughout Europe during the Christian <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>. Inspired by the preaching of a First Crusade, crusader mobs in France and Germany perpetrated the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland_massacres" title="Rhineland massacres">Rhineland massacres</a> of 1096, devastating Jewish communities along the Rhine River, including the <a href="/wiki/SHuM_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="SHuM cities">SHuM cities</a> of Speyer, Worms, and Mainz. The cluster of cities contain the earliest Jewish settlements north of the Alps, and played a major role in the formation of Ashkenazi Jewish religious tradition,<sup id="cite_ref-shum_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shum-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> along with Troyes and Sens in France. Nonetheless Jewish life in Germany persisted, while some Ashkenazi Jews joined Sephardic Jewry in Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-ashkenazimjvl_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ashkenazimjvl-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Expulsions from England (1290), France (1394), and parts of Germany (15th century), gradually pushed Ashkenazi Jewry eastward, to <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Poland" title="History of the Jews in Poland">Poland</a> (10th century), <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Lithuania</a> (10th century), and Russia (12th century). Over this period of several hundred years, some have suggested, Jewish economic activity was focused on trade, business management, and financial services, due to several presumed factors: <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> European prohibitions restricting certain activities by Jews, preventing certain financial activities (such as "<a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usurious</a>" loans)<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2019)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> between Christians, high rates of literacy, near universal male education, and ability of merchants to rely upon and trust family members living in different regions and countries.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rzeczpospolita.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Rzeczpospolita.png/220px-Rzeczpospolita.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Rzeczpospolita.png/330px-Rzeczpospolita.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Rzeczpospolita.png/440px-Rzeczpospolita.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1568" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rzeczpospolita.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth at its greatest extent.</div></div></div>
<p>By the 15th century, the Ashkenazi Jewish communities in Poland were the largest Jewish communities of the <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">Diaspora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenazim_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenazim-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> This area, which eventually fell under the domination of Russia, Austria, and <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> (Germany), would remain the main center of Ashkenazi Jewry until the <a href="/wiki/Holocaust" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.
</p><p>The answer to why there was so little assimilation of Jews in central and eastern Europe for so long would seem to lie in part in the probability that the alien surroundings in central and eastern Europe were not conducive, though there was some assimilation. Furthermore, Jews lived almost exclusively in <a href="/wiki/Shtetl" title="Shtetl">shtetls</a>, maintained a strong system of education for males, heeded rabbinic leadership, and had a very different lifestyle to that of their neighbours; and all of these tendencies increased with every outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_references">Medieval references</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wormsjews.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Wormsjews.jpg/220px-Wormsjews.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Wormsjews.jpg/330px-Wormsjews.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Wormsjews.jpg 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="297" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wormsjews.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jews from <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a> (Germany) wear the mandatory <a href="/wiki/Yellow_badge" title="Yellow badge">yellow badge</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the first half of the 11th century, <a href="/wiki/Hai_Gaon" title="Hai Gaon">Hai Gaon</a> refers to questions that had been addressed to him from Ashkenaz, by which he undoubtedly means Germany. <i><a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a></i> in the latter half of the 11th century refers to both the language of Ashkenaz<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and the country of Ashkenaz.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> During the 12th century, the word appears quite frequently. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Mahzor_Vitry" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahzor Vitry">Mahzor Vitry</a></i>, the kingdom of Ashkenaz is referred to chiefly in regard to the ritual of the synagogue there, but occasionally also with regard to certain other observances.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the literature of the 13th century, references to the land and the language of Ashkenaz often occur. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Solomon_ben_Aderet" class="mw-redirect" title="Solomon ben Aderet">Solomon ben Aderet</a>'s Responsa (vol. i., No. 395); the Responsa of <a href="/wiki/Asher_ben_Jehiel" title="Asher ben Jehiel">Asher ben Jehiel</a> (pp.&#160;4, 6); his <i>Halakot</i> (Berakot i. 12, ed. Wilna, p.&#160;10); the work of his son <a href="/wiki/Jacob_ben_Asher" title="Jacob ben Asher">Jacob ben Asher</a>, <i>Tur Orach Chayim</i> (chapter 59); the Responsa of Isaac ben Sheshet (numbers 193, 268, 270).
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a></i> compilation, <i>Genesis Rabbah</i>, Rabbi Berechiah mentions Ashkenaz, Riphath, and Togarmah as German tribes or as German lands. It may correspond to a <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word that may have existed in the Greek dialect of the Jews in <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Syria Palaestina</a>, or the text is corrupted from "Germanica". This view of Berechiah is based on the Talmud (Yoma 10a; Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 71b), where Gomer, the father of Ashkenaz, is translated by <i>Germamia</i>, which evidently stands for Germany, and which was suggested by the similarity of the sound.
</p><p>In later times, the word Ashkenaz is used to designate southern and western Germany, the ritual of which sections differs somewhat from that of eastern Germany and Poland. Thus the prayer-book of <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Horowitz" title="Isaiah Horowitz">Isaiah Horowitz</a>, and many others, give the <a href="/wiki/Piyyutim" class="mw-redirect" title="Piyyutim">piyyutim</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/Minhag" title="Minhag">Minhag</a> of Ashkenaz and Poland.
</p><p>According to 16th-century mystic <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Ba%27al_Shem_of_Chelm" class="mw-redirect" title="Elijah Ba&#39;al Shem of Chelm">Rabbi Elijah of Chelm</a>, Ashkenazi Jews lived in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> during the 11th century. The story is told that a German-speaking Jew saved the life of a young German man surnamed Dolberger. So when the knights of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> came to siege Jerusalem, one of Dolberger's family members who was among them rescued Jews in Palestine and carried them back to <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a> to repay the favor.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Further evidence of German communities in the holy city comes in the form of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakhic</a> questions sent from Germany to Jerusalem during the second half of the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span></h3>
<p>Material relating to the history of German Jews has been preserved in the communal accounts of certain communities on the Rhine, a <i>Memorbuch</i>, and a <i>Liebesbrief</i>, documents that are now part of the <a href="/wiki/David_Sassoon#Progeny" title="David Sassoon">Sassoon</a> Collection.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graetz" title="Heinrich Graetz">Heinrich Graetz</a> has also added to the history of German Jewry in modern times in the abstract of his seminal work, <i>History of the Jews</i>, which he entitled "Volksthümliche Geschichte der Juden."
</p><p>In an essay on Sephardi Jewry, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Elazar" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel Elazar">Daniel Elazar</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_Center_for_Public_Affairs" title="Jerusalem Center for Public Affairs">Jerusalem Center for Public Affairs</a><sup id="cite_ref-sephardic_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sephardic-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> summarized the demographic history of Ashkenazi Jews in the last thousand years. He notes that at the end of the 11th century, 97% of world Jewry was Sephardic and 3% Ashkenazi; in the mid-17th century, "Sephardim still outnumbered Ashkenazim three to two"; by the end of the 18th century, "Ashkenazim outnumbered Sephardim three to two, the result of improved living conditions in Christian Europe versus the Ottoman Muslim world."<sup id="cite_ref-sephardic_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sephardic-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> By 1930, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Ruppin" title="Arthur Ruppin">Arthur Ruppin</a> estimated that Ashkenazi Jews accounted for nearly 92% of world Jewry.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> These factors are sheer demography showing the migration patterns of Jews from Southern and Western Europe to Central and Eastern Europe.
</p><p>In 1740 a family from Lithuania became the first Ashkenazi Jews to settle in the Jewish Quarter of Jerusalem.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the generations after emigration from the west, Jewish communities in places like Poland, Russia, and Belarus enjoyed a comparatively stable socio-political environment. A thriving publishing industry and the printing of hundreds of biblical commentaries precipitated the development of the <a href="/wiki/Hasidic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasidic">Hasidic</a> movement as well as major Jewish academic centers.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> After two centuries of comparative tolerance in the new nations, massive westward emigration occurred in the 19th and 20th centuries in response to <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogroms</a> in the east and the economic opportunities offered in other parts of the world. Ashkenazi Jews have made up the majority of the <a href="/wiki/American_Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="American Jew">American Jewish</a> community since 1750.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashkenazim_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashkenazim-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the context of the European <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, Jewish emancipation began in 18th century France and spread throughout Western and Central Europe. <a href="/wiki/Disabilities_(Jewish)" title="Disabilities (Jewish)">Disabilities</a> that had limited the rights of Jews since the Middle Ages were abolished, including the requirements to wear distinctive clothing, pay special taxes, and live in <a href="/wiki/Jewish_ghettos_in_Europe" title="Jewish ghettos in Europe">ghettos</a> isolated from non-Jewish communities, and the prohibitions on certain professions. Laws were passed to integrate Jews into their host countries, forcing Ashkenazi Jews to adopt family names (they had formerly used <a href="/wiki/Patronymics" class="mw-redirect" title="Patronymics">patronymics</a>). Newfound inclusion into public life led to cultural growth in the <i><a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">Haskalah</a></i>, or Jewish Enlightenment, with its goal of integrating modern European values into Jewish life.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> As a reaction to increasing antisemitism and assimilation following the emancipation, <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a> was developed in central Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Other Jews, particularly those in the <a href="/wiki/Pale_of_Settlement" title="Pale of Settlement">Pale of Settlement</a>, turned to <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>. These tendencies would be united in <a href="/wiki/Labor_Zionism" title="Labor Zionism">Labor Zionism</a>, the founding ideology of the State of Israel.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Holocaust">The Holocaust</span></h4>
<p>Of the estimated 8.8 million Jews living in Europe at the beginning of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the majority of whom were Ashkenazi, about 6 million – more than two-thirds – were systematically murdered in <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a>. These included 3 million of 3.3 million <a href="/wiki/Polish_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish Jews">Polish Jews</a> (91%); 900,000 of 1.5 million in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> (60%); and 50–90% of the Jews of other Slavic nations, Germany, Hungary, and the Baltic states, and over 25% of the Jews in France. Sephardi communities suffered similar depletions in a few countries, including Greece, the Netherlands and the former Yugoslavia.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
As the large majority of the victims were Ashkenazi Jews, their percentage dropped from an estimate of 92% of world Jewry in made in 1930<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> to nearly 80% of world Jewry today. The Holocaust also effectively put an end to the dynamic development of the Yiddish language in the <a href="/wiki/Yiddish_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Yiddish Renaissance">previous decades</a>, as the vast majority of the Jewish victims of the Holocaust, around 5 million, were Yiddish speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Many of the surviving Ashkenazi Jews <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">emigrated</a> to countries such as Israel, Canada, Argentina, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Australia" title="History of the Jews in Australia">Australia</a>, and the United States after the war.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Following the Holocaust, some sources place Ashkenazim today as making up approximately 83–85 percent of Jews worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Sergio_DellaPergola" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergio DellaPergola">Sergio DellaPergola</a> in a rough calculation of <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>, implies that Ashkenazi make up a notably lower figure, less than 74%.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com.au_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com.au-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Other estimates place Ashkenazi Jews as making up about 75% of Jews worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khazzoom_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khazzoom-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews_in_Israel" title="Ashkenazi Jews in Israel">Ashkenazi Jews in Israel</a></div>
<p>In Israel, the term <i>Ashkenazi</i> is now used in a manner unrelated to its original meaning, often applied to all Jews who settled in Europe and sometimes including those whose ethnic background is actually Sephardic. Jews of any non-Ashkenazi background, including Mizrahi, Yemenite, Kurdish and others who have no connection with the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, have similarly come to be lumped together as Sephardic. Jews of mixed background are increasingly common, partly because of intermarriage between Ashkenazi and non-Ashkenazi, and partly because many do not see such historic markers as relevant to their life experiences as Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-meyers_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meyers-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious Ashkenazi Jews living in Israel are obliged to follow the authority of the chief Ashkenazi rabbi in <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakhic</a> matters. In this respect, a religiously Ashkenazi Jew is an Israeli who is more likely to support certain religious interests in Israel, including certain political parties. These political parties result from the fact that a portion of the Israeli electorate votes for Jewish religious parties; although the electoral map changes from one election to another, there are generally several small parties associated with the interests of religious Ashkenazi Jews. The role of religious parties, including small religious parties that play important roles as coalition members, results in turn from Israel's composition as a complex society in which competing social, economic, and religious interests stand for election to the <a href="/wiki/Knesset" title="Knesset">Knesset</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Unicameralism" title="Unicameralism">unicameral</a> legislature with 120 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ashkenazi Jews have played a prominent role in the economy, media, and politics<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> of Israel since its founding. During the first decades of Israel as a state, strong cultural conflict occurred between Sephardic and Ashkenazi Jews (mainly east European Ashkenazim). The roots of this conflict, which still exists to a much smaller extent in present-day Israeli society, are chiefly attributed to the concept of the "<a href="/wiki/Melting_pot" title="Melting pot">melting pot</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> That is to say, all Jewish immigrants who arrived in Israel were strongly encouraged to "melt down" their own particular exilic identities within the general social "pot" in order to become Israeli.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ashkenazi Chief Rabbis in the Yishuv and Israel include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Isaac_Kook" title="Abraham Isaac Kook">Abraham Isaac Kook</a>: (23 February 1921 – 1 September 1935)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Halevi_Herzog" class="mw-redirect" title="Isaac Halevi Herzog">Isaac Halevi Herzog</a>: (1937 – 25 July 1959)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isser_Yehuda_Unterman" title="Isser Yehuda Unterman">Isser Yehuda Unterman</a>: (1964–1972)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Goren" title="Shlomo Goren">Shlomo Goren</a>: (1972–1983)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avraham_Shapira" title="Avraham Shapira">Avraham Shapira</a>: (1983–1993)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Israel_Meir_Lau" class="mw-redirect" title="Israel Meir Lau">Israel Meir Lau</a>: (1993 – 3 April 2003)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/She%27ar_Yashuv_Cohen" title="She&#39;ar Yashuv Cohen">She'ar Yashuv Cohen</a> (acting): (3 April 2003 – 14 April 2003)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yona_Metzger" title="Yona Metzger">Yona Metzger</a>: (14 April 2003 – 14 August 2013)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Lau" title="David Lau">David Lau</a>: (14 August 2013 – present)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Who_is_a_Jew%3F" title="Who is a Jew?">Who is a Jew?</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_religion">By religion</span></h3>
<p>Religious Jews have <a href="/wiki/Minhag" title="Minhag">minhagim</a>, customs, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a>, or religious law, and different interpretations of law. Different groups of religious Jews in different geographic areas historically adopted different customs and interpretations. On certain issues, Orthodox Jews are required to follow the customs of their ancestors, and do not believe they have the option of picking and choosing. For this reason, observant Jews at times find it important for religious reasons to ascertain who their household's religious ancestors are in order to know what customs their household should follow. These times include, for example, when two Jews of different ethnic background marry, when a non-Jew converts to Judaism and determines what customs to follow for the first time, or when a lapsed or less observant Jew returns to traditional Judaism and must determine what was done in his or her family's past. In this sense, "Ashkenazic" refers both to a family ancestry and to a body of customs binding on Jews of that ancestry. <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a>, which does not necessarily follow those minhagim, did nonetheless originate among Ashkenazi Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a religious sense, an Ashkenazi Jew is any Jew whose family tradition and ritual follows Ashkenazi practice. Until the Ashkenazi community first began to develop in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, the centers of Jewish religious authority were in the Islamic world, at <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a>. Ashkenaz (Germany) was so distant geographically that it developed a <i><a href="/wiki/Minhag" title="Minhag">minhag</a></i> of its own. Ashkenazi Hebrew came to be pronounced in ways distinct from other forms of Hebrew.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In this respect, the counterpart of Ashkenazi is <a href="/wiki/Sephardic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sephardic">Sephardic</a>, since most non-Ashkenazi Orthodox Jews follow Sephardic rabbinical authorities, whether or not they are ethnically Sephardic. By tradition, a Sephardic or <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mizrahi">Mizrahi</a> woman who marries into an <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox</a> or <a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">Haredi</a> Ashkenazi Jewish family raises her children to be Ashkenazi Jews; conversely an Ashkenazi woman who marries a <a href="/wiki/Sephardi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sephardi">Sephardi</a> or Mizrahi man is expected to take on Sephardic practice and the children inherit a Sephardic identity, though in practice many families compromise. A <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">convert</a> generally follows the practice of the <a href="/wiki/Beth_din" title="Beth din">beth din</a> that converted him or her. With the integration of Jews from around the world in Israel, North America, and other places, the religious definition of an Ashkenazi Jew is blurring, especially outside <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>New developments in Judaism often transcend differences in religious practice between Ashkenazi and Sephardic Jews. In North American cities, social trends such as the <a href="/wiki/Chavurah" title="Chavurah">chavurah movement</a>, and the emergence of "post-denominational Judaism"<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> often bring together younger Jews of diverse ethnic backgrounds. In recent years, there has been increased interest in <i><a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">Kabbalah</a></i>, which many Ashkenazi Jews study outside of the <a href="/wiki/Yeshiva" title="Yeshiva">Yeshiva</a> framework. Another trend is the new popularity of <a href="/wiki/Ecstatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecstatic">ecstatic</a> worship in the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Renewal" title="Jewish Renewal">Jewish Renewal</a> movement and the <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_Carlebach_(musician)" title="Shlomo Carlebach (musician)">Carlebach</a> style <a href="/wiki/Minyan" title="Minyan">minyan</a>, both of which are nominally of Ashkenazi origin.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_culture">By culture</span></h3>
<table class="box-Multiple_issues plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple_issues compact-ambox" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><div class="mw-collapsible" style="width:95%; margin: 0.2em 0;"><b>This section has multiple issues.</b> Please help <b><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ashkenazi_Jews&amp;action=edit">improve it</a></b> or discuss these issues on the <b><a href="/wiki/Talk:Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Talk:Ashkenazi Jews">talk page</a></b>. <small><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove these template messages</a>)</i></small>
